The Prophet's Command to Seek Forgiveness: Not a Sin
Sanusi explains the verse 'Ask forgiveness for your sin' and defends prophetic infallibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two interpretations of the command to the Prophet to seek forgiveness, and how they preserve his infallibility.

Core Idea
The command to seek forgiveness does not imply sin; it is either for the community or a devotional act.

This passage from Sanusi addresses a potentially troubling verse: God commands the Prophet to ask forgiveness for his sin. How can a sinless prophet have a sin? Sanusi presents two interpretations that preserve the doctrine of infallibility while explaining the verse's deeper wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the two interpretations Sanusi offers.
Pay attention to the phrase 'increase in rank'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Find and note the two possible explanations: one involving the community, the other a devotional act. Understand the reasoning behind each.
The second interpretation includes multiple subtleties.
3
Understand the preferred view
Sanusi indicates the second interpretation is closer. Reflect on why it better preserves prophetic infallibility and what it teaches about grace.
Consider the connection to Surah Al-Fath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿وَاسْتَغْفِرْ لِذَنْبِكَ ﴾ [محمد : ١٩] : فقيل : إِنَّهُ خطاب له والمرادُ أُمَّتُه ، ويحتمل أن يكونَ أُمر بذلك على سبيل التعبدِ المحضِ ؛ زيادةً في رفع الدرجاتِ ، وتذكيراً لنعمة العصمة بطلب دوامها ، وإشارةً إلى أنّها محض فضل بلا وجوب ولا استحقاق . ونكتة إضافةِ الذنب إليه هنا : ما سبق في آية سورة ( الفتح ) ، وهذا الوجه أقربُ ، والله تعالى أعلم .
And as for His saying, the Exalted: 'And ask forgiveness for your sin' [Muhammad 47:19]: It was said: It is an address to him, but the intent is his community. And it is possible that he was commanded to do that purely as an act of worship, to increase rank, and to remind of the blessing of infallibility (al-‘iṣma) by requesting its continuation, and as an indication that it is pure grace without obligation or merit. And the subtlety of attributing sin to him here is what preceded in the verse of Surah Al-Fath. And this view is closer, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a potentially problematic verse (Surah Muhammad 47:19) where the Prophet is commanded to ask forgiveness for his sin. The author provides two interpretations: (1) it is addressed to the Prophet but meant for his community, so the sin is not his; (2) it is a purely devotional command to increase his rank and to remind that his infallibility is a grace from God, not something earned. The author notes that the subtlety of attributing 'sin' to him here is similar to what was said in Surah Al-Fath, and considers this second interpretation closer to the truth.

عصمة
ذنب

Correction: Sanusi shows the command is either for the community or a devotional act that actually confirms his sinlessness by emphasizing that his infallibility is a gift from God.

Key Takeaway

Sanusi reinterprets a Quranic phrase as honorific.

Learning Objective

Core Idea
Divine words to prophets are often honorific, not reprimands.

When Allah says to His Prophet 'May Allah pardon you,' some might mistakenly think it signals a fault. But Sanusi clarifies that this is a form of honor and respect, similar to how we address great people with polite phrases. This lesson unpacks that interpretation.

3

He compares it to saying 'May Allah rectify you and honor you' when beginning speech with dignitaries. This shows the phrase is a polite opening.
This analogy helps reframe the meaning.

Many assume 'pardon' implies wrongdoing. Sanusi corrects this by showing it's a respectful formula.

Verse on Spoils: No Blame on the Prophet
Sanusi explains that the verse refers to a previous decree, not a reprimand, and that the address is not to the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse about spoils (Quran 8:68) refers to a prior decree permitting spoils, not a reprimand, and that the address in verse 8:67 does not include the Prophet or his elite companions.

Core Idea
The spoils verse honors the Prophet, not rebukes him.

This passage from Sanusi tackles a common misinterpretation of two verses from Surah Al-Anfal regarding the spoils of Badr. Many readers might think the verses imply that the Prophet and his Companions sinned by taking spoils before divine permission. Sanusi clarifies that the 'prior decree' mentioned is actually God's permission, making the verse a commendation of the Prophet's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the two Quranic verses (8:68 and 8:67) that Sanusi explains.
Focus on the phrases 'كتاب سبق' (a prior decree) and 'معاتبة' (reproach).
2
Identify the two clarifications
Sanusi makes two main points: first, the 'prior decree' in verse 8:68 refers to God's permission of spoils for this ummah; second, the address in verse 8:67 is not directed at the Prophet or the elite companions.
3
Understand the reasoning
Notice how Sanusi connects verse 8:69 ('So eat from what you have gained as lawful and good') to support his interpretation. Also note the hadith about spoils being lawful only for Prophet Muhammad.
This shows the broader context of the verse.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding: that the verse implies sin. Sanusi directly refutes this by clarifying that it is about a prior decree and a unique favor.
This lesson guards against a wrong belief about the Prophet's infallibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ( لَوْلَا كِتَبٌ مِّنَ اللَّهِ سَبَقَ لَمَسَّكُمْ فِيمَا أَخَذْتُمْ عَذَابُ عظيم [الأنفال : ٦٨] : فالأظهرُ أنَّ معناه : لولا كتابٌ مِنَ اللهِ سبقَ بإحلال الغنائم لكم، وتخصيصكم بهذه الفضيلة دونَ مَنْ قبلكم .. لكانَ كذا وكذا، ولهذا قال تعالى : ﴿ فَكُلُوا مِمَّا غَنِمْتُمْ حَلَالًا طَيِّبًا [الأنفال : ٦٩]، فليس في الآيةِ إلزام ذنبٍ ولا معاتبة، بل فيها ذِكْرُ ما خُصَّ بهِ نبينا وسيدنا ومولانا محمَّدٌ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ وفُضْلَ بِهِ مِنْ بين سائر الأنبياء والرسل على جميعِهم الصلاةُ والسلام، فكأنَّهُ تعالى قال : ما كان هذا لنبي غيرِكَ، كما قال عليه الصلاةُ والسلام : أُحِلَّتْ لِيَ الْغَنَائِمُ وَلَمْ تَحِلَّ لِنَبِيُّ قَبْلِي. والخطاب بقولِهِ تعالى : ﴿ تُرِيدُونَ عَرَضَ الدُّنْيَا ﴾ [الأنفال : ٦٧] إِنَّما هو لِمَنْ أرادَ مِنَ الناسِ ذلكَ وتَجرَّدَ غرضُهُ لعَرَضِ الدنيا وحدَهُ ، وللاستكثار منها ، وليس المراد به النبيَّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ ولا علية أصحابه رضيَ الله تعالى عن جميعهم.
And as for His saying, the Exalted: 'If not for a decree from Allah that preceded, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took' [Al-Anfal 8:68]: The most apparent meaning is: If not for a decree from Allah that preceded by permitting spoils for you, and specifying you with this virtue over those before you... it would have been such and such. And for this reason He said: 'So eat from what you have gained as lawful and good' [Al-Anfal 8:69]. So there is no imputation of sin nor reproach in the verse; rather, in it is a mention of what our Prophet, our Master, our Mawla Muhammad (peace be upon him) was specified with, and the virtue he was given above all other prophets and messengers (peace be upon them all). It is as if He said: 'This was not for any prophet other than you,' just as he (peace and blessings be upon him) said: 'Spoils were made lawful for me, and they were not made lawful for any prophet before me.' And the address in His saying: 'You desire the goods of this world' [Al-Anfal 8:67] is only for those among the people who desired that and whose sole purpose was the goods of this world and to amass them. The Prophet (peace be upon him) and the elite of his companions (may Allah be pleased with them all) are not intended.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of two related verses about the Battle of Badr. The author first addresses verse 8:68, which literally says 'if not for a prior decree, a great punishment would have touched you for what you took.' He explains that the 'prior decree' is God's permission of spoils for this community. Thus, the verse does not accuse the Prophet or the Companions of sin, but rather highlights the virtue of the Prophet in being permitted spoils, which no previous prophet had. The author then addresses verse 8:67, where the address 'You desire the goods of this world' is clarified to refer not to the Prophet or the leading Companions, but only to those whose sole aim was worldly gain. Thus, no blame attaches to the Prophet.

Key Terms
كتاب سبق
غنائم
معاتبة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 8:68 implies that the Prophet and Companions sinned by taking spoils before permission.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the 'prior decree' is God's permission, so the verse acknowledges a special favor, not a sin. He says: 'There is no imputation of sin nor reproach in the verse; rather, it is a mention of what our Prophet was specified with.'

Checkpoint
What is the 'prior decree' (kitābun min Allahi sabaqa) in the interpretation of verse 8:68?
A) A decree of punishment for the disbelievers.
B) A decree permitting spoils for this Ummah.
C) A decree about the creation of the universe.
D) A decree about the prophethood of Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
The verse about spoils is not a reproach but a declaration of the Prophet's unique honor, as it refers to a prior divine decree permitting spoils for his community alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse that seems to criticize someone, do you quickly assume the worst? How might you train yourself to first seek a more honorable interpretation, especially regarding the Prophet and the righteous? Commit to one specific method (e.g., asking a scholar, checking tafsir) before forming a negative opinion.
?
The Prophet was given a unique honor when the apparent context could have been seen as a rebuke. Think of a situation in your life where you felt blamed or punished. Is it possible that Allah is actually granting you an unrecognized honor or opportunity? Write down one such trial and reframe it as a potential blessing. What would change in your response to it?
?
The verse limits the address 'You desire the goods of this world' to those whose sole aim was worldly gain. Examine your own intentions today. In what area of your life—work, study, or relationships—is your sole aim worldly gain? What one concrete change can you make tonight to purify that intention for Allah alone?

The Verse 'He Frowned and Turned Away' is Not a Sin
Sanusi quotes Qadi Iyad to argue that the frown was not a sin but a lesson for others.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Prophet's frown in Surah Abasa was not a sin but an act of obedience.

Core Idea
The Prophet's frown was guidance, not guilt.

The opening verses of Surah Abasa describe the Prophet turning away from a blind man to speak to a wealthy disbeliever. Some have misunderstood this as a divine rebuke. Qadi Iyad clarifies that the verse does not indicate a sin; rather, it informs the disbeliever and shows that the Prophet's action was obedience to Allah. Let's examine his argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the key terms: عبس, تولى, استئلاف.
2
Identify Qadi Iyad's main claim
Find the statement that directly says there is no sin. Understand that the verse is informing, not blaming.
The claim is: 'ليس فيه إثبات ذنب له عليه السلام'.
3
Understand the reasoning
The passage explains that the better course would have been to attend to the blind man, but the Prophet's actual action was still obedience—conveying the message and seeking to win over the disbeliever as Allah legislated.
This shows that the verse points out what was better, not that the Prophet sinned.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Many think this verse rebukes the Prophet. Qadi Iyad corrects this: it is a lesson for others and a clarification of priorities, not a blame.

وأما قوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى ...) [عبس: ١) الآية : فقال عياض « الشفا » : ليسَ فِيهِ إثباتُ ذنبٍ لهُ عليهِ السلامُ ، بل إعلامُ اللهِ ذلك المتصدى لهُ ممَّنْ لا يزَّكَّى ، وأنَّ الصواب والأولى كان لو كُشِفَ لك حال الرجلين لاخترت الإقبال على الأعمى ، وفعلُ النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لِمَا فعل ، وتصدّيهِ لذلك الكافر .. كان طاعة الله ، وتبليغاً عنه ، واستئلافاً له كما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى له ، لا معصية ومخالفة لهُ.

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the opening verses of Surah Abasa, where the Prophet frowned and turned away from a blind man to attend to a wealthy disbeliever. The author quotes Qadi Iyad from his work 'Al-Shifa' to argue that the verse does not prove any sin on the Prophet. Instead, it informs the person who was seeking guidance and would not purify himself. The better course was to attend to the blind man, but the Prophet's actual action (turning to the disbeliever) was still obedience to God—it was an act of conveying the message and seeking to win over the disbeliever as Allah had commanded. Thus, there was no disobedience.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's frown was not a sin but an act of obedience in conveying Allah's message and seeking to win over a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Is there someone in your life—a difficult relative, a colleague, or a stranger—whom you avoid because you think they are 'not worth' your effort? ?

The Verse of Spoils Is Not a Rebuke but a Grant of Favor
Sanusi explains that the permission of spoils is a special favor, not a rebuke.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse permitting spoils is a special favor for the Prophet Muhammad, not a rebuke for sin.

Core Idea
Spoils were a divine favor for Muhammad, not a rebuke.

This passage addresses a common misinterpretation of the spoils verse. Sanusi clarifies that the permission is actually a unique honor bestowed upon the Prophet, distinguishing him from all previous prophets. The goal is to preserve the impeccable character of the Prophet and his elite companions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the author's main claim.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that Sanusi states there is no imposition of sin or rebuke in the verse. This is the central thesis.
3
Examine the supporting hadith
The hadith 'Spoils were made lawful for me...' reinforces that this is a favor unique to Muhammad.
4
Understand the second verse clarification
Sanusi explains that the rebuke in verse 67 is for those who sought worldly gain, not for the Prophet or his elite companions.
5
Reflect on prophetic impeccability
Consider why this clarification is important for maintaining the perfect character of the Prophet.

And therefore He says: 'So eat of what you have taken as spoils, lawful and good' (Al-Anfal 69). There is no imposition of sin or rebuke in the verse; rather, it is mention of what our Prophet and our Master Muhammad (peace be upon him) was singled out with and favored with over all other prophets and messengers (peace and blessings be upon them all). So it is as if He said: This was not for any prophet other than you, just as he (peace and prayer be upon him) said: 'Spoils were made lawful for me, and they were not made lawful for any prophet before me.' (1) And the address in His saying: 'You desire the goods of this world' (Al-Anfal 67) is only for whoever among the people desired that and whose aim was solely the goods of this world and seeking much of it; and the Prophet (peace be upon him) is not intended by it, nor the elite of his Companions (may God be pleased with them all).

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the Prophet from the implication that he sinned by taking spoils. The author first explains that the verse permitting spoils is actually a special favor granted to Muhammad, not a rebuke. The phrase 'There is no imposition of sin or rebuke in the verse' directly counters the objection. The author then reinforces this by quoting the hadith that spoils were made lawful only for him. The second paragraph addresses the verse 'You desire the goods of this world,' clarifying that it is directed at those who sought worldly gain purely, and not at the Prophet or his leading Companions. The author thus preserves the impeccable righteousness of the Prophet and his elite followers.

The Verse of 'Abasa Does Not Imply Sin
Sanusi presents two interpretations of 'Abasa to deny any sin by the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two classical interpretations of Surah 'Abasa that defend the Prophet from any implication of sin.

Core Idea
The verse of 'Abasa actually defends the Prophet, not blames him.

The verse of 'Abasa is sometimes misunderstood as rebuking the Prophet for frowning at a blind man. However, classical scholars like Qadi Iyad have provided clear explanations showing that the verse does not imply sin. This lesson presents two such interpretations: one from Qadi Iyad and another attributing the action to the disbeliever.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its full literal translation. Note the key terms mentioned and the overall argument.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا إثبات ذنب' – no establishment of sin.
2
Analyze Qadi Iyad's argument
Identify how Qadi Iyad explains the verse: he states that it does not establish a sin but rather informs the Prophet of a better choice, and that the Prophet's action was actually obedience to God.
Notice the use of 'بل إعلام' – rather it is an informing.
3
Consider the alternative interpretation
Read the second interpretation attributed to Abu Tammam, which suggests that 'he who frowned' refers to the disbeliever, not the Prophet. This completely removes any possible blame.
This shows that even the wording can be interpreted differently to avoid any implication of sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله جلَّ مِنْ قائل : عَبَسَ وَتَوَلَّى ...) [عبس: ١) الآية : فقال عياض الشفا » : ( ليسَ فِيهِ إثباتُ ذنبٍ لهُ عليهِ السلامُ ، بل إعلامُ اللهِ ذلك المتصدى لهُ ممَّنْ لا يزَّكَّى ، وأنَّ الصواب والأولى كان لو كُشِفَ لك حال الرجلين لاخترت الإقبال على الأعمى ، وفعلُ النبيِّ صلَّى اللهُ عليهِ وسلَّمَ لِمَا فعل ، وتصدّيهِ لذلك الكافر .. كان طاعة الله، وتبليغاً عنه، واستئلافاً له كما شرعَهُ اللهُ تعالى له، لا معصية ومخالفة لهُ

وما قَصَّهُ الله تعالى عليهِ مِنْ ذلكَ إعلام بحال الرجلين، وتوهين أمر الكافر عندَه، والإشارة إلى الإعراض عنه بقولِهِ : ﴿وَمَا عَلَيْكَ أَلَّا يَزَّكَّى ) (عبس: ٧) .

وقيل : أَرادَ بـ « عبس وتولَّى » : الكافر الذي كانَ معَ النبي صلَّى الله عليه وسلَّمَ، قاله أبو تمام ((۲)
As for His saying, May He be glorified: 'He frowned and turned away' (Abasa: 1) – the verse: Qadi Iyad said in al-Shifa: 'There is no establishment of sin for him (peace be upon him) in it. Rather, it is God informing him of the state of that which he addressed, who will not purify himself, and that the correct and better course was that had the case of the two men been disclosed to you, you would have chosen to face the blind man. And the action of the Prophet (peace be upon him) in what he did, and his addressing that disbeliever, was obedience to God, conveyance from Him, and winning him over as God had legislated for him; it was not disobedience or opposition to Him. And what God recounted to him of that is an informing of the state of the two men, and an undermining of the matter of the disbeliever in his view, and an indication to turn away from him by His saying: 'And what is upon you if he does not purify himself' (Abasa: 7). And it was said: He intended by 'He frowned and turned away' the disbeliever who was with the Prophet (peace be upon him). Abu Tammam said this.

Commentary
This passage presents two defenses against the implication that the Prophet sinned when he frowned at the blind man. First, the author quotes Qadi Iyad's explanation that the verse does not establish a sin, but rather indicates that the better choice would have been to attend to the blind man. The Prophet's action was actually obedience, not sin. The second part of the verse is said to undermine the disbeliever. Then an alternative interpretation is given: that 'he who frowned' actually refers to the disbeliever, not the Prophet. This alternate view, attributed to Abu Tammam, completely removes any possible blame from the Prophet.

Adam's Disobedience: Linguistic vs Legal Sin
Sanusi distinguishes linguistic and legal sin in Adam's case and cites Ibn al-Arabi on tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience and how it defends prophetic infallibility in the case of Adam.

Core Idea
Adam's 'sin' was linguistic, not legal—a distinction that preserves his infallibility.

The Quranic verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and fell into error' (Taha: 121) may seem to imply that Adam committed a sin. However, scholars of kalam and tafsir have clarified that this refers to linguistic disobedience, not legal sin. In this lesson, we explore this critical distinction and its role in safeguarding the prophetic principle of 'isma (infallibility).

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the full Arabic passage and its English translation to get the author's complete argument.
Notice the incomplete quote at the end; we will discuss its significance.
2
Identify the two types of disobedience
Look for where the author defines 'linguistic disobedience' and 'legal disobedience'. Note the key differences.
Linguistic disobedience includes forgetfulness and misinterpretation; legal disobedience requires intentionality and knowledge of prohibition.
3
Understand the defended doctrinal point
Ask yourself: why does the author go to such lengths to argue that Adam's action was not legal disobedience? This safeguards the belief that prophets are infallible from deliberate sin.
4
Reflect on the incomplete citation
The passage begins a quote from Ibn al-Arabi on the necessity of declaring prophets transcendent (tanzih). Consider how this reinforces the argument.
The full quote is not provided here, but the point is clear: prophets must be respected above acts of disobedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَعَصَى عَادَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى ﴾ [طه : ۱۲۱] : فالتحقيق أنَّ المرادَ : المعصية والغوايةُ اللغويتانِ؛ وهما وقوع صورة المخالفة والغواية التي هي تركُ المراشد، سواء وقعا عمداً أو نسياناً أو تأويلاً، لا الشرعيتان؛ وهما المخالفة عمداً معَ العلم بالتحريم، فإنَّ المخالفة على هذه الصفة لم تقع مِنْ آدمَ عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإِنَّما وقعَتْ منه نسياناً أو بالتأويل، وذلك مبسوط في « الشفا » وكتب التفسير (۳)

ويرحم الله تعالى الإمام العالم ابن العربي حيث قال : ( يجب تنزيه
As for His saying: 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and fell into error' (Taha: 121): the verified position is that what is intended is the linguistic disobedience and error, which are the occurrence of the form of contravention and error which is abandoning the right path, whether they occur intentionally, forgetfully, or by misinterpretation; not the legal disobedience and error, which are contravention intentionally with knowledge of prohibition. For contravention in this form did not occur from Adam (peace be upon him); rather it occurred from him forgetfully or by misinterpretation. This is elaborated in al-Shifa and the books of exegesis (3). And may God have mercy on the Imam, the scholar Ibn al-Arabi, where he said: 'It is necessary to declare transcendent...'
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage defends the prophetic infallibility of Adam by distinguishing two types of disobedience: linguistic (لغوية) and legal (شرعية). The author asserts that the verse describes linguistic disobedience, not the legal one that involves intentional rebellion. Adam's action was not a deliberate sin but an error (نسيان) or misinterpretation (تأويل). The author cites other works for further detail. The passage concludes with an incomplete quote from Ibn al-Arabi on the necessity of declaring prophets transcendent (تنزيه).

المعصية الشرعية
الغواية
التأويل
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Adam committed a deliberate sin deserving punishment, which would compromise his prophetic infallibility.
Correction: Adam's act was not a legal sin but a linguistic one—an error without moral blame, as it was done forgetfully or by misinterpretation.

Checkpoint

B) Between linguistic and legal disobedience
C) Between pre-Islamic and Islamic prophets

Key Takeaway
The distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience preserves Adam's infallibility by showing his action was an error, not a deliberate sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Two Interpretations of 'He Frowned and Turned Away'
Two interpretations of the verse 'Abasa wa Tawalla'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of the phrase 'Abasa wa Tawalla' and how they relate to the Prophet.

Core Idea
The verse 'Abasa wa Tawalla' has two interpretations, one involving the disbeliever.

In this passage, the author discusses a key Qur'anic verse that is often misunderstood. He presents two interpretations: one derived from the context and another attributed to Abu Tammam. This helps clarify the proper understanding of the Prophet's conduct.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text and translation

Adam's Disobedience: Linguistic vs. Legal
Sanusi clarifies the Qur'anic verse about Adam's disobedience using linguistic vs. legal distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between linguistic and legal disobedience and how it preserves prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
Adam's 'disobedience' was a linguistic misstep, not a legal sin.

The Qur'an states that Adam disobeyed his Lord. Does this contradict the belief that prophets are infallible? In this passage, Sanusi clarifies that the term 'disobedience' used for Adam is linguistic, not legal. This distinction resolves the apparent conflict and upholds the prophetic stature of Adam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the terms 'linguistic' and 'legal'.
The passage begins by quoting the verse.
2
Identify the two categories
Find where Sanusi defines 'linguistic disobedience' and 'legal disobedience'. Note the conditions for each.
Linguistic: any act of opposition, even forgetfully. Legal: intentional with knowledge of prohibition.
3
Apply to Adam
Understand why Adam's action was only linguistic: it occurred forgetfully or by interpretation, not intentionally with full knowledge.
4
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself how this distinction protects the doctrine of prophetic infallibility (ismah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿ وَعَصَى آدَمُ رَبَّهُ فَغَوَى﴾ : فالتحقيق أن المراد: المعصية والغواية اللغويتان؛ وهما وقوع صورة المخالفة والغواية التي هي ترك المراشد، سواء وقعا عمداً أو نسياناً أو تأويلاً، لا الشرعيتان؛ وهما المخالفة عمداً مع العلم بالتحريم، فإن المخالفة على هذه الصفة لم تقع من آدم عليه الصلاة والسلام، وإنما وقعت منه نسياناً أو بالتأويل، وذلك مبسوط في « الشفا » وكتب التفسير.
As for His statement, the Exalted: {And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred} [Ta-Ha: 121]: The verification is that what is intended is the linguistic disobedience and error – they are the occurrence of the form of opposition and error which is leaving the right course, whether they occur intentionally, forgetfully, or by interpretation – not the legal [disobedience and error]; they are opposition intentionally with knowledge of prohibition. For opposition in this manner did not occur from Adam, peace be upon him. It only occurred from him forgetfully or by interpretation. And that is expounded in 'al-Shifa' and books of tafsir.
Source pages p. 215

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the verse 'And Adam disobeyed his Lord and erred.' The author distinguishes between linguistic and legal meanings of 'disobedience' and 'error'. Linguistically, any act that goes against a command or misses the right path can be called disobedience or error, regardless of intent. Legally, disobedience requires intentional violation with knowledge that the act is prohibited. Adam's action was not legal disobedience because it occurred through forgetfulness or mistaken interpretation. Thus, his prophetic infallibility is maintained.

Key Terms
المعصية اللغوية
المعصية الشرعية
الغواية
آدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic statement 'Adam disobeyed his Lord' proves that Adam committed a sin, contradicting prophetic infallibility.
Correction: The disobedience attributed to Adam is linguistic, meaning any act of opposition irrespective of intent, not legal disobedience which requires intentional violation with knowledge of prohibition. Since Adam's act was forgetful or interpretive, it does not constitute a sin.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why was Adam's disobedience not legal disobedience?
A) Because he did not actually eat from the tree.
B) Because it occurred forgetfully or by interpretation, not intentionally with knowledge of prohibition.
C) Because it was commanded by Allah.
D) Because it was a minor sin.

Key Takeaway
Adam's prophetic infallibility is preserved because his 'disobedience' was linguistic, not legal—it occurred forgetfully or by interpretation, not as intentional rebellion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you unknowingly did something wrong. Did you immediately assume guilt or seek to understand the context? How does Adam's forgetfulness challenge your tendency to judge yourself or others harshly?
?
If you were Adam, would you have accepted the label of 'disobedient' without justifying your intention? Are there areas in your life where you insist on your innocence even when your actions appear wrong?
?
This lesson shows that even a prophet can be misunderstood. When you hear criticism of religious figures, do you rush to defend or dismiss, or do you pause to investigate the deeper meaning? What would it take for you to adopt the same careful approach as Sanusi?

Ibn al-'Arabi on Adam's Intentional Act and Forgetfulness
Ibn al-'Arabi defends prophetic infallibility by analyzing Adam's sin as intentional but forgetful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn al-'Arabi reconciles Adam's deliberate eating from the tree with his forgetfulness of the covenant, and why only Allah may use such language about prophets.

Core Idea
Adam's disobedience was intentional but his covenant was forgotten, allowing a unique divine reproach.

The great scholar Ibn al-'Arabi offers a subtle defense of prophetic infallibility by distinguishing between Adam's intentional act of eating and his forgetting of the divine command. He shows how the Qur'an itself uses both 'disobeyed' and 'forgot' to address these aspects, and then clarifies that humans must not repeat this language except in recitation. This passage deepens our understanding of how to speak about the prophets with proper reverence.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn al-'Arabi's statement
Carefully read the Arabic quote from Ibn al-'Arabi and its translation. Notice how he begins by affirming the necessity of exalting prophets above accusations of ignorance.
The quote is within parentheses in the text.
2
Identify the two aspects of Adam's act
Ibn al-'Arabi explains that Adam was intentional in eating (the act itself was deliberate) but forgetful of the covenant (the prohibition). Find the Qur'anic verses he cites for each aspect.
The verse 'And Adam disobeyed' refers to the intentional act; 'but he forgot' refers to his excuse.
3
Understand the divine perspective
Reflect on Ibn al-'Arabi's point that Allah, as Lord, may say 'He disobeyed' as a reproach and 'he forgot' as a favor. This is permissible only for Allah, not for humans.
The term 'tathrib' means reproach, and 'taqrib' means approximation or drawing near.
4
Apply the lesson to our speech
Note the concluding rule: we may only mention such things about prophets when reciting the Qur'an or quoting the Prophet. This teaches us to guard our tongues regarding the status of prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويرحم الله تعالى الإمام العالم ابن العربي حيث قال: ( يجب تنزيه الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام عما نسب إليهم الجهال، ولكن البارئ سبحانه وتعالى بحكمه النافذ وقضائه السابق أسلم آدم إلى الأكل من الشجرة متعمداً للأكل ناسياً للعهد؛ فقال في تعمده: ﴿وَعَصَى آدَمُ﴾، وقال في بيان عذره: ﴿وَلَقَدْ عَهِدْنَا إِلَى آدَمَ مِن قَبْلُ فَنَسِيَ﴾، فمتعلق العمد غير متعلق النسيان، وجاز للمولى تبارك وتعالى أن يقول في عبده لحقه: «عصى» تثريباً، ويعود عليه بفضله فيقول: «نسي» تقريباً، ولا يجوز لأحد منا أن يطلق ذلك على آدم أو يذكره إلا في تلاوة القرآن، أو قول النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم). وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا جَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا﴾: فقال الواحدي في ( تفسيره ): ( إن إبليس أتى حواء في غير صورته التي عرفته، وقال لها: ما الذي في بطنك؟ قالت: ما أدري، قال: إني أخاف أن يكون بهيمة أو كلباً أو خنزيراً، وذكرت ذلك لآدم، فلم يزالا في هم من ذلك، ثم أتاها وقال: إن سألت الله تعالى أن يجعله بشراً سوياً مثلك... تسميه عبد الحارث؟ وكان اسم إبليس في الملائكة الحارث، فلم يزل بها حتى غرها، فلما ولدت ولداً سوياً... سمت عبد الحارث برضا آدم عليه السلام؛ وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا ولداً بشراً سوياً... وجَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ﴾ يعني: إبليس، فأوقع الجمع موقع الواحد فيما آتاهما من الولد.
And may Allah have mercy on the eminent scholar Ibn al-'Arabi when he said: 'It is obligatory to exalt the prophets, peace be upon them, above what the ignorant have attributed to them. However, the Creator, exalted be He, by His penetrating decree and prior determination, surrendered Adam to eat from the tree – intentionally for eating, forgetful of the covenant. So He said regarding his intention: {And Adam disobeyed}, and He said regarding the explanation of his excuse: {And We had already made a covenant with Adam before, but he forgot}. So the object of intention is different from the object of forgetfulness. And it is permissible for the Lord, blessed and exalted, to say regarding His servant, due to His right, 'He disobeyed' as a reproach, and He returns to him with His favor saying 'he forgot' as an approximation. And it is not permissible for any of us to say that about Adam or mention it except in recitation of the Qur'an or the saying of the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace.' And as for His statement, the Exalted: {Then when He gave them a righteous [child], they ascribed to Him partners in that which He gave them}: al-Wahidi said in his Tafsir: 'Indeed, Iblis came to Eve in a form other than his known one and said to her: "What is in your belly?" She said: "I do not know." He said: "I fear it may be an animal, a dog, or a pig." She mentioned that to Adam, and they remained in worry about that. Then he came to her and said: "If you ask Allah to make him a sound human like you... will you name him 'Abd al-Harith?" And the name of Iblis among the angels was al-Harith. He kept at her until he deceived her. Then when she gave birth to a sound child... she named him 'Abd al-Harith with the approval of Adam, peace be upon him. That is the statement of the Exalted: {Then when He gave them a righteous [child], they ascribed to Him partners} meaning Iblis. So He used the plural in place of the singular concerning what He gave them of the child.'
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage contains two parts. First, the author cites Ibn al-'Arabi to further explain Adam's disobedience. Ibn al-'Arabi emphasizes that while Adam intentionally ate (the act was deliberate), he was forgetful of the covenant (the prohibition). Therefore, the Qur'an uses 'disobeyed' for the intentional act and 'forgot' as an excuse. This is permissible for Allah to say regarding His servant, but no human may use such language about Adam except when reciting the Qur'an or quoting the Prophet. Second, the author addresses the verse about Adam and Eve naming their child 'Abd al-Harith (servant of al-Harith). Al-Wahidi explains that Iblis deceived Eve into naming the child after him, under the pretense of gratitude. The verse uses the plural 'they ascribed partners' even though only Eve named the child, because Adam consented. This exonerates Adam and Eve from actual shirk, as the naming was done out of ignorance and deception.

Key Terms
ابن العربي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Adam's sin was purely accidental or that he did not sin at all.
Correction: The passage clarifies that Adam intentionally ate but forgot the covenant, so there was both intention and forgetfulness.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn al-'Arabi, why does the Qur'an use both 'disobeyed' and 'forgot' for Adam?
A) To show that Adam sinned and then repented.
B) To distinguish between the intentional eating and the forgotten covenant.
C) To indicate that Adam was forced to eat.
D) To demonstrate that Adam was not a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-'Arabi defends prophetic infallibility by showing that Adam's disobedience was intentional but his forgetfulness was an excuse, and only Allah may combine reproach and favor in such speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin intentionally, do you truly believe that Allah can turn even your deliberate disobedience into an occasion for closeness—or do you assume your sin has permanently distanced you from Him? How will you act on that belief today?
?
Adam forgot the covenant; what covenant from Allah have you 'forgotten' that you now need to remember and honor, even if your past action was deliberate?
?
If only Allah has the right to combine reproach and favor in speaking of sin, what does that mean for how you speak about others' sins—especially those you consider intentional? Will you restrain your own tongue and leave judgment to Him?

The Naming of 'Abd al-Harith: Deception, Not Shirk
Al-Wahidi explains the verse about ascribing partners through the story of Iblis's deception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Adam and Eve naming their child 'Abd al-Harith, and how this incident does not constitute shirk but was a result of Iblis's deception.

Core Idea
The naming was a deception by Iblis, not actual polytheism.

The passage presents al-Wahidi's commentary on the Qur'anic verse about Adam and Eve ascribing partners to Allah. Through a detailed narrative, al-Wahidi explains that Iblis deceived Eve into naming her child 'Abd al-Harith (servant of al-Harith, Iblis's angelic name), and Adam consented. This clarifies that the verse refers to a misguided action born of ignorance and deceit, not intentional shirk. This lesson helps us understand how the Qur'an can use strong language while still exonerating the prophets from actual sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and al-Wahidi's explanation
Begin by reading the Qur'anic verse and then al-Wahidi's account of the event. Note how Iblis disguises himself and sows doubt about the child's health.
The verse is: {Then when He gave them a righteous [child], they ascribed to Him partners in that which He gave them}.
2
Follow the narrative of deception
Trace the steps: Iblis comes to Eve in a different form, expresses fear about the child, then suggests that if she asks Allah for a sound child, she should name it 'Abd al-Harith. Iblis's angelic name was al-Harith, so this is a form of associating gratitude to Iblis.
The name 'Abd al-Harith means 'servant of al-Harith'.
3
Understand the plural 'they'
Al-Wahidi explains that the verse uses the plural 'they ascribed partners' even though only Eve named the child, because Adam consented. This implicates both, but the action was done under deception.
The Qur'an uses the plural to include Adam's approval.
4
Reflect on the exoneration
The passage shows that the naming was not done out of worship of Iblis but out of gratitude misdirected due to deception. This preserves the infallibility of Adam and Eve from actual shirk.

Source Text

Adam's Disobedience and Forgetfulness Reconciled
Sanusi distinguishes between Adam's intentional act and forgetfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi reconciles the verses about Adam's disobedience and forgetfulness to preserve prophetic infallibility.

Core Idea
God can reproach and then cover with mercy; Adam's disobedience and forgetfulness refer to different aspects.

In this passage, Sanusi addresses a common objection: how can Adam be a prophet if he disobeyed God? He carefully distinguishes between the intentional act of eating and the forgetting of the covenant, showing that God's assessment allows for both reproach and mercy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let Sanusi's own argument come through.
Notice that Sanusi uses the Quranic verses as his primary evidence.
2
Identify the two Quranic verses
Find the references to Adam disobeying (from Ta-Ha 121) and Adam forgetting (from Ta-Ha 115). See how Sanusi uses them.
3
Understand the distinction
Sanusi says the object of intention is different from the object of forgetfulness. Make sure you see that one refers to the act of eating, the other to the covenant.
This distinction resolves the apparent contradiction.
4
Note the prohibition
Sanusi says we cannot call Adam disobedient except when reciting the Quran or quoting the Prophet. This is an important practical guideline.
This safeguards prophetic respect.

الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام عما نسب إليهم الجُهَّالُ، ولكن الباري سبحانه وتعالى بحكمه النافذ وقضائه السابق أسلم آدم إلى الأكل من الشجرة متعمداً للأكل ناسياً للعهد؛ فقال في تعمده: وَعَصَى آدَمُ، وقال في بيان عذره: ﴿وَلَقَدْ عَهِدْنَا إِلَىٰ آدَمَ مِن قَبْلُ فَنَسِيَ﴾، فمُتعلَّقُ العمد غير متعلَّق النسيان، وجاز للمولى تبارك وتعالى أن يقول في عبده لحقه: «عصى» تثريباً(١)، ويعود عليه بفضله فيقول: «نَسِيَ» تقريباً، ولا يجوز لأحد منا أن يُطلق ذلك على آدم أو يذكره إلا في تلاوة القرآن، أو قول النبي ﷺ(٢). وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا جَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا﴾ [الأعراف: ١٩٠]: فقال الواحدي في (تفسيره): (إن إبليس أتى حواء في غير صورته التي عرفته، وقال لها: ما الذي في بطنك؟ قالت: ما أدري، قال: إني أخاف أن يكون بهيمة أو كلباً أو خنزيراً، وذكرت ذلك لآدم، فلم يزالا في هم من ذلك، ثم أتاها وقال: إن سألت الله تعالى أن يجعله بشراً سوياً مثلك... تُسميه عبد الحارث؟ وكان اسم إبليس في الملائكة الحارث، فلم يزل بها حتى غرها، فلما ولدت ولداً سوياً... سمته عبد الحارث برضا آدم عليه السلام؛ وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا وَلَدًا بَشَرًا سَوِيًّا... وَجَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا﴾ إذ سَمَّياه عبد الحارث، ولا ينبغي أن يكون عبداً إلا الله، ولم تعرف حواء أنه إبليس، ولم يكن هذا شركاً بالله؛ لأنهما لم يذهبا إلى أن الحارث ربهما، لكنهما قصدا إلى أنه كان سبب نجاته، وتم الكلام عند قوله: ﴿آتَاهُمَا﴾ ثم ذكر كفار مكة فقال: ﴿فَتَعَالَى اللَّهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾ (انتهى)(١). قلت: قال ابن العربي في «الأحكام» في توهين هذا القول وتزييفه: (وهذا القول ونحوه مذكور في ضعيف الحديث في «الترمذي» وغيره، وفي الإسرائيليات التي ليس لها ثبات، ولا يُعوِّلُ عليها من له قلب، والقول الأشبه بالحق: أن المراد بهذا جنس الآدميين)(٢). وأما قول آدم عليه السلام: ﴿رَبَّنَا ظَلَمْنَا أَنفُسَنَا﴾ [الأعراف: ٢٣]: فقول صدر منه على سبيل الاستكانة والتعظيم لجناب أوامر المولى تبارك وتعالى ومناهيه؛ بحيث يَحِق على العبيد أن لو كان الأمر بأيديهم ألا بوجه من الوجوه، لا عمداً ولا نسياناً، ولا بانتهاك مخالفة تقع منهم ولا بتأويل، وأشار عليه السلام بذلك إلى أنه لا حجة للعبد على سيده، ولا يعتذر لنفسه فيما خالف من أمره تعالى ونهيه، ولا حق له.

(١) التثريب: اللوم.
(٢) انظر «أحكام القرآن» لابن العربي (٢٥٩/٣).
(١) انظر «الوجيز» (ص ٤٢٥).
(٢) انظر «أحكام القرآن» (٢/٣٥٥).
The prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, from what the ignorant have attributed to them. But the Creator, glorified and exalted, by His penetrating decree and prior judgment, delivered Adam to eating from the tree, intentionally eating, forgetting the covenant. So He said regarding his intention: 'And Adam disobeyed,' and He said regarding the clarification of his excuse: 'And We had already taken a covenant from Adam before, but he forgot.' So the object of intention is not the object of forgetfulness. It is permissible for the Master, blessed and exalted, to say concerning His servant of His right: 'He disobeyed' as a reproach, and He returns to him with His favor and says: 'He forgot' as an approximation. It is not permissible for any of us to say that about Adam or mention it except in reciting the Quran or the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And as for His saying, exalted: 'Then when He gave them a righteous [child], they made partners for Him in what He had given them' [al-A'raf: 190] - al-Wahidi said in his Tafsir: 'Indeed Iblis came to Eve in other than his form that she knew, and said to her: What is in your belly? She said: I do not know. He said: I fear it may be a beast, a dog, or a pig. She mentioned that to Adam, and they did not cease to be worried about that. Then he came to her and said: If you ask Allah, exalted, to make him a sound human like you... would you name him 'Abd al-Harith? And the name of Iblis among the angels was al-Harith. He did not cease with her until he deceived her. When she gave birth to a sound child... she named him 'Abd al-Harith with the consent of Adam, peace be upon him. That is His saying, exalted: 'Then when He gave them a righteous child, a sound human... they made partners for Him in what He had given them' because they named him 'Abd al-Harith. It is not fitting that any be a servant except Allah. Eve did not know that it was Iblis. This was not polytheism towards Allah because they did not intend that al-Harith is their lord, but they intended that he was the cause of his salvation. The speech is completed at His saying: 'He gave them,' then He mentioned the disbelievers of Mecca and said: 'Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him].' (End.) I say: Ibn al-'Arabi said in 'al-Ahkam' in weakening this saying and falsifying it: 'This saying and the like are mentioned in weak hadith in al-Tirmidhi and others, and in the Isra'iliyyat which have no stability, and one who has a heart does not rely on them. The saying more resembling the truth is that what is meant by this is the genus of human beings.' And as for the saying of Adam, peace be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [al-A'raf: 23] - it is a saying issued from him in a manner of humility and magnification of the majesty of the commands and prohibitions of the Master, blessed and exalted, such that it is incumbent upon servants that if the matter were in their hands, they would not do anything at all: neither intentionally nor forgetfully, nor by committing a violation that occurs from them, nor by interpretation. He, peace be upon him, indicated by that that there is no argument for the servant against his master, and he does not excuse himself regarding what he has opposed of His command and prohibition, and he has no right.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the prophets (specifically Adam and Eve) against accusations of sin based on apparent meanings of Quranic verses. The author first addresses the verse about Adam's disobedience and forgetfulness (Ta-Ha 115 and 121). He explains that God's statement that Adam 'disobeyed' refers to the intentional act of eating, while 'he forgot' refers to the covenant; the two statements are not contradictory because they concern different aspects. God may reproach His servant for a right due to Him, yet also cover him with mercy by calling it forgetfulness. However, we as humans are not allowed to label Adam with 'disobedience' except when reciting the Quran or repeating the Prophet's words. Next, the author discusses the verse about Adam and Eve making partners for God (al-A'raf 190) by reporting al-Wahidi's interpretation: Iblis tricked Eve into naming their child 'Abd al-Harith (thinking he was a righteous angel), but they did not consider al-Harith a god; it was only a name signifying that he was a cause of the child's safety, not true polytheism. The author then quotes Ibn al-'Arabi who weakens this narrative as weak hadith and Isra'iliyyat, and states that the verse actually refers to the descendants of Adam in general (i.e., people who are given children and then commit shirk). Finally, the author interprets Adam's statement 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' as an expression of extreme humility and reverence for God's commands, not an admission of actual sin. Adam is showing that a servant has no excuse before his Master, even if the apparent action is not sinful. The overall point is that the prophets are innocent of the sins attributed to them by the ignorant.

Key Terms
عصيان
نسيان
تثريب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Quran contradicts itself by saying Adam both disobeyed and forgot.
Correction: The two statements refer to different objects (act vs. covenant), so there is no contradiction.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it not contradictory that God said both 'Adam disobeyed' and 'Adam forgot'?
A) Because one statement is abrogated by the other.
B) Because the object of intention is different from the object of forgetfulness.
C) Because the word 'disobeyed' refers to Eve, not Adam.
D) Because the verse is metaphorical and not to be taken literally.

Key Takeaway
Adam's actions are interpreted by God from two angles: intentional disobedience and excusable forgetfulness, preserving his prophetic status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you used the excuse of forgetfulness to dismiss a deliberate sin? How does this passage challenge you to face your intentional disobedience without hiding behind excuses?
?
If God can reproach Adam for His right and then cover with mercy, what does that mean for the way you treat your own failures? Will you let God's mercy be greater than your guilt, or do you cling to self-condemnation?
?
This passage shows that a prophet can have a real, intentional sin that God still covers. How does that change your view of your own slip-ups? Are you willing to trust that God can use you despite your faults, as He did with Adam?

The 'Abd al-Harith Narrative and Its Refutation
Sanusi reports and then refutes a weak interpretation of al-A'raf 190.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the weak tradition about Adam and Eve naming their child 'Abd al-Harith and why scholars reject it.

Core Idea
The 'Abd al-Harith story is weak and based on Isra'iliyyat; the verse actually refers to idolaters among Adam's descendants.

Sanusi presents a controversial interpretation from al-Wahidi involving Iblis tricking Eve into naming their child 'Abd al-Harith. He then quotes Ibn al-'Arabi's decisive refutation, showing that such narratives are unreliable and that the true meaning of the verse is general.

Guided Path
1
Read the narrative from al-Wahidi
Read the story of Iblis and Eve as reported by al-Wahidi. Notice the details: Iblis comes in disguise, suggests naming the child 'Abd al-Harith, and Adam consents.
This story is presented to explain the verse but is later criticized.
2
Identify the theological problem
The story implies that Adam and Eve named their child 'Abd al-Harith, which could be seen as associating a partner with God (shirk). Sanusi notes that they did not intend shirk, but Ibn al-'Arabi rejects the whole narrative.
3
Read Ibn al-'Arabi's critique
Ibn al-'Arabi says this story is found in weak hadith and Isra'iliyyat, which are unreliable. He gives the correct interpretation: the verse refers to the descendants of Adam (humanity) who commit shirk.
This is the scholarly consensus.
4
Understand the correct interpretation
The verse 'they make partners for Him' refers to later polytheists, not Adam and Eve. The prophets are innocent.

According to Ibn al-'Arabi, what is the most correct interpretation of the verse 'they made partners for Him'?

?

Adam's Statement 'We Have Wronged Ourselves' as Humility
Sanusi interprets Adam's statement as humility and reverence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Adam's confession of wrongdoing is an expression of profound humility and reverence, not an admission of actual sin.

Core Idea
Adam's 'wronging ourselves' shows a servant's total submission, not an actual sin.

The final part of the passage addresses Adam's Quranic plea 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves.' Sanusi explains that this is not an admission of sin but a demonstration of the servant's complete humility before God's commands, indicating that even if there is no actual fault, the servant has no excuse.

Guided Path
1
Read Adam's statement and Sanusi's explanation
Read the passage where Sanusi interprets Adam's words 'We have wronged ourselves' (al-A'raf 23).
Pay attention to the terms 'استكانة' (humility) and 'تعظيم' (magnification).
2
Understand the context of humility
Sanusi says Adam spoke out of humility and reverence for God's commands. Even if an action is not sinful, a servant should show complete submission.
3
Realize the protection of Prophetic infallibility
Since prophets are sinless, Adam's words cannot be an admission of actual sin. They serve as a model of how a servant should address God.
4
Summarize the key point
Adam's statement is a rhetorical expression of humility, not a confession of guilt.

الأنبياء عليهم الصلاة والسلام عما نسب إليهم الجُهَّالُ، ولكن الباري سبحانه وتعالى بحكمه النافذ وقضائه السابق أسلم آدم إلى الأكل من الشجرة متعمداً للأكل ناسياً للعهد؛ فقال في تعمده: وَعَصَى آدَمُ، وقال في بيان عذره: ﴿وَلَقَدْ عَهِدْنَا إِلَىٰ آدَمَ مِن قَبْلُ فَنَسِيَ﴾، فمُتعلَّقُ العمد غير متعلَّق النسيان، وجاز للمولى تبارك وتعالى أن يقول في عبده لحقه: «عصى» تثريباً(١)، ويعود عليه بفضله فيقول: «نَسِيَ» تقريباً، ولا يجوز لأحد منا أن يُطلق ذلك على آدم أو يذكره إلا في تلاوة القرآن، أو قول النبي ﷺ(٢). وأما قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا جَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا﴾ [الأعراف: ١٩٠]: فقال الواحدي في (تفسيره): (إن إبليس أتى حواء في غير صورته التي عرفته، وقال لها: ما الذي في بطنك؟ قالت: ما أدري، قال: إني أخاف أن يكون بهيمة أو كلباً أو خنزيراً، وذكرت ذلك لآدم، فلم يزالا في هم من ذلك، ثم أتاها وقال: إن سألت الله تعالى أن يجعله بشراً سوياً مثلك... تُسميه عبد الحارث؟ وكان اسم إبليس في الملائكة الحارث، فلم يزل بها حتى غرها، فلما ولدت ولداً سوياً... سمته عبد الحارث برضا آدم عليه السلام؛ وذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا آتَاهُمَا صَالِحًا وَلَدًا بَشَرًا سَوِيًّا... وَجَعَلَا لَهُ شُرَكَاءَ فِيمَا آتَاهُمَا﴾ إذ سَمَّياه عبد الحارث، ولا ينبغي أن يكون عبداً إلا الله، ولم تعرف حواء أنه إبليس، ولم يكن هذا شركاً بالله؛ لأنهما لم يذهبا إلى أن الحارث ربهما، لكنهما قصدا إلى أنه كان سبب نجاته، وتم الكلام عند قوله: ﴿آتَاهُمَا﴾ ثم ذكر كفار مكة فقال: ﴿فَتَعَالَى اللَّهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ﴾ (انتهى)(١). قلت: قال ابن العربي في «الأحكام» في توهين هذا القول وتزييفه: (وهذا القول ونحوه مذكور في ضعيف الحديث في «الترمذي» وغيره، وفي الإسرائيليات التي ليس لها ثبات، ولا يُعوِّلُ عليها من له قلب، والقول الأشبه بالحق: أن المراد بهذا جنس الآدميين)(٢). وأما قول آدم عليه السلام: ﴿رَبَّنَا ظَلَمْنَا أَنفُسَنَا﴾ [الأعراف: ٢٣]: فقول صدر منه على سبيل الاستكانة والتعظيم لجناب أوامر المولى تبارك وتعالى ومناهيه؛ بحيث يَحِق على العبيد أن لو كان الأمر بأيديهم ألا بوجه من الوجوه، لا عمداً ولا نسياناً، ولا بانتهاك مخالفة تقع منهم ولا بتأويل، وأشار عليه السلام بذلك إلى أنه لا حجة للعبد على سيده، ولا يعتذر لنفسه فيما خالف من أمره تعالى ونهيه، ولا حق له.

(١) التثريب: اللوم.
(٢) انظر «أحكام القرآن» لابن العربي (٢٥٩/٣).
(١) انظر «الوجيز» (ص ٤٢٥).
(٢) انظر «أحكام القرآن» (٢/٣٥٥).
The prophets, peace and blessings be upon them, from what the ignorant have attributed to them. But the Creator, glorified and exalted, by His penetrating decree and prior judgment, delivered Adam to eating from the tree, intentionally eating, forgetting the covenant. So He said regarding his intention: 'And Adam disobeyed,' and He said regarding the clarification of his excuse: 'And We had already taken a covenant from Adam before, but he forgot.' So the object of intention is not the object of forgetfulness. It is permissible for the Master, blessed and exalted, to say concerning His servant of His right: 'He disobeyed' as a reproach, and He returns to him with His favor and says: 'He forgot' as an approximation. It is not permissible for any of us to say that about Adam or mention it except in reciting the Quran or the saying of the Prophet, peace be upon him. And as for His saying, exalted: 'Then when He gave them a righteous [child], they made partners for Him in what He had given them' [al-A'raf: 190] - al-Wahidi said in his Tafsir: 'Indeed Iblis came to Eve in other than his form that she knew, and said to her: What is in your belly? She said: I do not know. He said: I fear it may be a beast, a dog, or a pig. She mentioned that to Adam, and they did not cease to be worried about that. Then he came to her and said: If you ask Allah, exalted, to make him a sound human like you... would you name him 'Abd al-Harith? And the name of Iblis among the angels was al-Harith. He did not cease with her until he deceived her. When she gave birth to a sound child... she named him 'Abd al-Harith with the consent of Adam, peace be upon him. That is His saying, exalted: 'Then when He gave them a righteous child, a sound human... they made partners for Him in what He had given them' because they named him 'Abd al-Harith. It is not fitting that any be a servant except Allah. Eve did not know that it was Iblis. This was not polytheism towards Allah because they did not intend that al-Harith is their lord, but they intended that he was the cause of his salvation. The speech is completed at His saying: 'He gave them,' then He mentioned the disbelievers of Mecca and said: 'Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him].' (End.) I say: Ibn al-'Arabi said in 'al-Ahkam' in weakening this saying and falsifying it: 'This saying and the like are mentioned in weak hadith in al-Tirmidhi and others, and in the Isra'iliyyat which have no stability, and one who has a heart does not rely on them. The saying more resembling the truth is that what is meant by this is the genus of human beings.' And as for the saying of Adam, peace be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [al-A'raf: 23] - it is a saying issued from him in a manner of humility and magnification of the majesty of the commands and prohibitions of the Master, blessed and exalted, such that it is incumbent upon servants that if the matter were in their hands, they would not do anything at all: neither intentionally nor forgetfully, nor by committing a violation that occurs from them, nor by interpretation. He, peace be upon him, indicated by that that there is no argument for the servant against his master, and he does not excuse himself regarding what he has opposed of His command and prohibition, and he has no right.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the prophets (specifically Adam and Eve) against accusations of sin based on apparent meanings of Quranic verses. The author first addresses the verse about Adam's disobedience and forgetfulness (Ta-Ha 115 and 121). He explains that God's statement that Adam 'disobeyed' refers to the intentional act of eating, while 'he forgot' refers to the covenant; the two statements are not contradictory because they concern different aspects. God may reproach His servant for a right due to Him, yet also cover him with mercy by calling it forgetfulness. However, we as humans are not allowed to label Adam with 'disobedience' except when reciting the Quran or repeating the Prophet's words. Next, the author discusses the verse about Adam and Eve making partners for God (al-A'raf 190) by reporting al-Wahidi's interpretation: Iblis tricked Eve into naming their child 'Abd al-Harith (thinking he was a righteous angel), but they did not consider al-Harith a god; it was only a name signifying that he was a cause of the child's safety, not true polytheism. The author then quotes Ibn al-'Arabi who weakens this narrative as weak hadith and Isra'iliyyat, and states that the verse actually refers to the descendants of Adam in general (i.e., people who are given children and then commit shirk). Finally, the author interprets Adam's statement 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' as an expression of extreme humility and reverence for God's commands, not an admission of actual sin. Adam is showing that a servant has no excuse before his Master, even if the apparent action is not sinful. The overall point is that the prophets are innocent of the sins attributed to them by the ignorant.

Key Terms
استكانة
تعظيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adam actually sinned by eating from the tree, and his confession 'we have wronged ourselves' proves it.
Correction: Adam's statement is an expression of humility and reverence, not an admission of actual sin, consistent with prophetic infallibility.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why did Adam say 'We have wronged ourselves'?
A) He actually committed a sin and was repenting.
B) To show humility and reverence for God's commands, despite being sinless.
C) He was quoting what Iblis told him to say.
D) He forgot that he was a prophet.

Key Takeaway
Adam's confession is a model of humility, not an admission of sin.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you fall short in obeying Allah, do you instinctively reach for excuses—'I forgot,' 'I didn't mean to,' 'I interpreted it differently'—rather than adopting Adam's posture of utter humility? How would your relationship with Allah change if you stopped arguing and simply said, 'I have no excuse before You'?
?
Consider a moment when you felt justified in a sin because you thought your intention was good or your circumstances were special. Does Adam's model—where he does not even claim a valid interpretation—expose a hidden pride in your heart? What would it cost you to drop every excuse and stand before Allah with nothing but humility?
?
Adam's statement magnifies the majesty of God's commands. The next time you are tempted to downplay a prohibition, will you remember that the Creator's command is so great that even an unintentional violation is not to be excused? Can you honestly say you tremble at every command, or have you become comfortable with selective obedience? What will you do to change that today?

Defending Adam and Hawwa from Shirk: The Naming of 'Abd al-Harith
Author defends Adam and Hawwa from shirk and cites Ibn al-Arabi's critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the act of Adam and Hawwa naming their son 'Abd al-Harith does not constitute shirk, according to the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
Intent determines shirk; means are not lords.

This passage addresses a potential misunderstanding about the prophetic couple Adam and Hawwa. Some might think that by naming their child 'Abd al-Harith (servant of the cultivator), they committed shirk. The author clarifies their true intention and defends them from this accusation, also citing Ibn al-Arabi's critique of weak narrations used to support such claims.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the source text carefully. Notice how the author directly addresses the naming incident and provides his reasoning.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لم يكن هذا شركا بالله'.
2
Understand the core argument against shirk
The author explains that Adam and Hawwa did not consider al-Harith as a lord, only as a cause of salvation. Reflect on why this distinction matters in defining shirk.
Shirk is about intent, not mere naming.
3
Examine Ibn al-Arabi's critique
Ibn al-Arabi dismisses the narration linking this event as weak hadith or Isra'iliyyat. Consider the methodological point: not all reports about prophets are reliable.
This reinforces the defense by questioning the source.
4
Apply the lesson
Think about how this reasoning protects prophets from false accusations and teaches us to evaluate reports critically.
The principle: means of salvation ≠ object of worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ سمَّياه عبد الحارثِ ، ولا ينبغي أن يكون عبداً إلا الله ، ولم تَعرِفْ حواء أنَّهُ إبليس ، ولم يكن هذا شِرْكاً باللهِ ؛ لأنَّهُما لم يذهبا إلى أنَّ الحارث ربُّهما ، لكنَّهما قصدا إلى أنَّهُ كانَ سبب نجاتِهِ ، وتم الكلامُ عند قوله : واتَنهُمَا ﴾ ، ثمَّ ذكرَ كُفَّارَ مكَّةَ فقالَ : فَتَعَلَى اللَّهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ ( انتهى ! (۱) قلتُ : قالَ ابنُ العربي في الأحكام » في توهين هذا القول وتزييفه : ( وهذا القولُ ونحوه مذكور في ضعيف الحديث في الترمذي » وغيرِه ، وفي الإسرائيليات التي ليس لها ثبات ، ولا يُعوِّلُ عليها مَنْ له قلب ، والقولُ الأشبه بالحقِّ : أنَّ المراد بهذا جنس الآدميين ((٢) . وأما قولُ آدم عليهِ السلامُ : رَبَّنَا ظَلَمْنَا أَنفُسَنَا [الأعراف : ٢٣] : فقول ﴾ صدرَ منه على سبيل الاستكانة والتعظيم لجنابِ أوامر المولى تبارك وتعالى ومناهيهِ ؛ بحيثُ يَحِقُ على العبيدِ أنْ لو كان الأمر بأيديهم ألا بوجهِ مِنَ الوجوه ، لا عمداً ولا نسياناً ، ولا بانتهاك مخالفة تقع منهم ولا بتأويل ، وأشار عليهِ السلامُ بذلكَ إلى أنَّهُ لا حُجَّةَ للعبدِ على سيّدِهِ ، ولا يَعتذِرُ لنفسِهِ فيما خالفَ مِنْ أمرِهِ تعالى ونهيه ، ولا حق لهُ على المولى العظيم أن يَعذِرَهُ بنسيان أو تأويل ، بلِ الحُجَّةُ للمولى تبارك وتعالى على كلِّ حالٍ ، وحكمه على عبدِهِ بأَنَّهُ معذور في بعض الأحوال.. محض فضل منه جلَّ وعلا ، ولهُ أنْ يُعذِّبَ مَنْ يَشَاءُ ويرحم مَنْ يَشَاءُ ، وهو المحمودُ المُنزَّهُ عَنِ النقص والظُّلْمِ على كلِّ حالٍ
When they named him 'Abd al-Harith, while it is not befitting that anyone be a servant except Allah, and Hawwa did not know that it was Iblis, and this was not shirk with Allah because they did not intend that al-Harith was their lord, but they intended that he was the cause of their salvation. And the speech is completed at His saying 'and He gave them both [the tree]' then He mentioned the disbelievers of Mecca and said 'Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him]' (end). I said: Ibn al-Arabi said in al-Ahkam regarding weakening this statement and refuting it: 'This statement and the like are mentioned in weak hadith in al-Tirmidhi and others, and in Isra'iliyyat that have no stability, and one who has a heart does not rely on them. The statement most resembling the truth is that what is meant by this is the generality of Adam's children.' And as for the saying of Adam, peace be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [al-A'raf 23] – it is a saying that issued from him by way of humility and veneration of the commands of the Sovereign, Blessed and Exalted, and His prohibitions; such that it becomes incumbent upon the servants that if the matter were in their hands, they would not do it in any way, neither intentionally nor forgetfully, nor by violating a prohibition occurring from them nor by interpretation. And he, peace be upon him, indicated by that that there is no proof for the servant against his Master, and he does not excuse himself in what he has contravened of His command and prohibition, and he has no right upon the Great Master to excuse him by forgetfulness or interpretation. Rather, the proof belongs to the Sovereign, Blessed and Exalted, in every case, and His judgment upon His servant that he is excused in some circumstances is pure grace from Him, Glorious and Exalted, and He has the right to punish whom He wills and have mercy on whom He wills, and He is Praiseworthy, Transcendent above deficiency and injustice in every case.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the prophets Adam and Hawwa from accusations of shirk and sin, and an interpretation of Adam's statement in the Quran. The author begins by refuting the claim that naming their child 'Abd al-Harith constituted shirk, explaining that they only considered al-Harith a means of salvation, not a lord. He then references a tafsir that connects this to the disbelievers of Mecca. Following this, the author mentions Ibn al-Arabi's critique of such narrations as weak or based on Isra'iliyyat. The core of the passage is the explanation of Adam's verse 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves.' The author argues that this is not an admission of sin but an expression of profound humility and recognition of God's sovereignty. He explains that Adam's words emphasize that the servant has no claim against his Master, and any excuse for failing to obey perfectly is purely by God's grace. The passage concludes by affirming God's absolute freedom to punish or forgive, and His transcendence above any deficiency or injustice.

Key Terms
عبد الحارث
شرك
إسرائيليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that Adam and Hawwa committed shirk by naming their child 'Abd al-Harith.
Correction: The author argues they did not intend shirk, seeing al-Harith only as a means, not a deity; furthermore, the narrations are weak.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why was naming their child 'Abd al-Harith not shirk?
A) Because Hawwa did not know it was Iblis
B) Because they considered al-Harith a cause, not a lord
C) Because the Quran explicitly permits it
D) Because Ibn al-Arabi said so

Key Takeaway
Adam and Hawwa's naming of 'Abd al-Harith was not shirk because they saw al-Harith as a means, not a deity, and the narrations implying otherwise are unreliable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's action as shirk without knowing their true intention? How does the example of Adam and Hawwa challenge your quick judgments? What would it take for you to give others the same benefit of the doubt in matters of faith?
?
Do you have any habits where you rely on 'causes' (like people or material means) almost as if they have independent power? How does this lesson's distinction between cause and Lord force you to re-examine where your trust truly lies?

Interpreting Adam's Confession: Humility, Not Admission of Sin
Author explains Adam's Quranic confession as humility and veneration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's interpretation of Adam's Quranic statement 'Rabbana zalamna anfusana' as an expression of humility and divine sovereignty, not an admission of sin.

Core Idea
Adam's confession is humility, not sin.

Adam's words in the Quran, 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves,' might seem like an admission of sin. The author explains that this saying was an act of profound humility and reverence for God's commands, emphasizing that the servant has no standing before the Master. This interpretation protects the prophetic status of Adam while affirming God's absolute sovereignty and grace.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Carefully read the source text. Notice how the author describes the manner in which Adam's statement was made.
Key phrase: 'على سبيل الاستكانة والتعظيم'
2
Understand the concept of 'istikanah'
The author says the statement was 'by way of humility and veneration.' Reflect on how this differs from admitting a sinful act.
Humility acknowledges God's greatness, not necessarily wrongdoing.
3
Recognize the implication about divine sovereignty
Adam's words show that the servant has no claim against God; any excuse (forgetfulness, interpretation) is not a right but pure grace. Consider how this relates to God's absolute authority.
The lesson: God is justified in all His judgments.
4
Connect to the broader theological point
The author concludes that God is free to punish or forgive, and is beyond deficiency. See how this interpretation safeguards God's justice and mercy.
This passage ties into predestination and divine justice.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذ سمَّياه عبد الحارثِ ، ولا ينبغي أن يكون عبداً إلا الله ، ولم تَعرِفْ حواء أنَّهُ إبليس ، ولم يكن هذا شِرْكاً باللهِ ؛ لأنَّهُما لم يذهبا إلى أنَّ الحارث ربُّهما ، لكنَّهما قصدا إلى أنَّهُ كانَ سبب نجاتِهِ ، وتم الكلامُ عند قوله : واتَنهُمَا ﴾ ، ثمَّ ذكرَ كُفَّارَ مكَّةَ فقالَ : فَتَعَلَى اللَّهُ عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ ( انتهى ! When they named him 'Abd al-Harith, while it is not befitting that anyone be a servant except Allah, and Hawwa did not know that it was Iblis, and this was not shirk with Allah because they did not intend that al-Harith was their lord, but they intended that he was the cause of their salvation. And the speech is completed at His saying 'and He gave them both [the tree]' then He mentioned the disbelievers of Mecca and said 'Exalted is Allah above what they associate [with Him]' (end). I said: Ibn al-Arabi said in al-Ahkam regarding weakening this statement and refuting it: 'This statement and the like are mentioned in weak hadith in al-Tirmidhi and others, and in Isra'iliyyat that have no stability, and one who has a heart does not rely on them. The statement most resembling the truth is that what is meant by this is the generality of Adam's children.' And as for the saying of Adam, peace be upon him: 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves' [al-A'raf 23] – it is a saying that issued from him by way of humility and veneration of the commands of the Sovereign, Blessed and Exalted, and His prohibitions; such that it becomes incumbent upon the servants that if the matter were in their hands, they would not do it in any way, neither intentionally nor forgetfully, nor by violating a prohibition occurring from them nor by interpretation. And he, peace be upon him, indicated by that that there is no proof for the servant against his Master, and he does not excuse himself in what he has contravened of His command and prohibition, and he has no right upon the Great Master to excuse him by forgetfulness or interpretation. Rather, the proof belongs to the Sovereign, Blessed and Exalted, in every case, and His judgment upon His servant that he is excused in some circumstances is pure grace from Him, Glorious and Exalted, and He has the right to punish whom He wills and have mercy on whom He wills, and He is Praiseworthy, Transcendent above deficiency and injustice in every case.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the prophets Adam and Hawwa from accusations of shirk and sin, and an interpretation of Adam's statement in the Quran. The author begins by refuting the claim that naming their child 'Abd al-Harith constituted shirk, explaining that they only considered al-Harith a means of salvation, not a lord. He then references a tafsir that connects this to the disbelievers of Mecca. Following this, the author mentions Ibn al-Arabi's critique of such narrations as weak or based on Isra'iliyyat. The core of the passage is the explanation of Adam's verse 'Our Lord, we have wronged ourselves.' The author argues that this is not an admission of sin but an expression of profound humility and recognition of God's sovereignty. He explains that Adam's words emphasize that the servant has no claim against his Master, and any excuse for failing to obey perfectly is purely by God's grace. The passage concludes by affirming God's absolute freedom to punish or forgive, and His transcendence above any deficiency or injustice.

Key Terms
استكانة
حجة
محض فضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Adam's confession 'Rabbana zalamna anfusana' is an admission of sin, implying he committed a wrongdoing.
Correction: The author clarifies it is an expression of humility and recognition of divine sovereignty, not an admission of sin.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does Adam's statement 'Rabbana zalamna anfusana' primarily express?
A) An admission of sin and regret
B) Humility and veneration of God's commands
C) A request for forgiveness
D) A justification for his actions

Key Takeaway
Adam's confession is a profound act of humility, affirming that the servant has no proof against his Master and that any excuse is pure grace from God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make a mistake, do you tend to excuse yourself by saying 'I didn't know' or 'I meant well'? How does Adam's refusal to lean on such excuses challenge the way you normally respond to your own faults?
?
Are there areas of your life where you secretly feel God owes you understanding or leniency because of your good intentions? Would you be willing to let go of that sense of entitlement and see any leniency as pure grace?
?
If you truly believed you have no right to be excused by forgetfulness or interpretation, how would your prayer of repentance change tonight? Would it become more desperate, more humble, more reliant on God's mercy alone?
